{"uri": "2024-05-349807143", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "05:58:07", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T05:58:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T05:30:24Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9490196108818054, "url": "https://www.itv.com/news/meridian/2024-05-09/uk-toddler-first-in-world-to-have-hearing-restored-on-new-gene-therapy-trial", "title": "UK toddler first in world to have hearing restored on new gene therapy trial", "body": "An 18-month-old girl from Oxfordshire has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, said the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said, \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" said Mrs Sandy, \"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\"\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon. Some 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\"\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\"\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels. I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding, \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years. Prof Bance said, \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\"\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really. It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe toddler is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding, \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\"\n\nThe treatment was currently not available on the NHS.\n\nWant a quick and expert briefing on the biggest news stories? Listen to our latest podcasts to find out What You Need To Know...\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\"", "source": {"uri": "itv.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "ITV Hub"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in {"uri": "8118284286", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "01:22:05", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T01:22:05Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T01:21:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9411764740943909, "url": "https://www.indy100.com/news/british-girl-has-hearing-restored-as-first-patient-on-new-gene-therapy-trial", "title": "British girl has hearing restored as first patient on new gene therapy trial", "body": "Opal Sandy, who was born completely deaf because of a rare genetic condition (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\nA British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nWhen (Opal) first turned (her head to loud clapping), I couldn't believe it. I thought it was a fluke ... but I repeated it a few times. I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\n(The trial) marks a new era in the treatment for deafness. It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nAs a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy. The treatment was currently not available on the NHS.\n\n\"What's really helped though is that the NHS does pay for genetic testing now for hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued.\n\n\"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\nThis trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\n\n\"The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to expand the range of treatments available.\"\n\nHealth and Social Care Secretary Victoria Atkins said: \"I am delighted and proud of what this government-backed trial has done for Opal, and what it could eventually do for children like her across the world.\n\n\"It marks an historic milestone as we develop a cure for this kind of deafness.\"", "source": {"uri": "indy100.com", "dataType": "news", {"uri": "8119015275", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "11:13:32", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T11:13:32Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T11:12:43Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://www.dailysabah.com/life/british-toddler-hears-for-1st-time-as-1st-patient-on-gene-therapy/news", "title": "British toddler hears for 1st time as 1st patient on gene therapy", "body": "A pioneering gene therapy trial has successfully restored hearing for a British girl, making her the inaugural participant worldwide to benefit from this breakthrough treatment.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition of auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses traveling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one-and-done\" gene therapy being trialed in the U.K. and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene. This gene is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin, which enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene in her right ear during surgery last Sept.\n\nHer parents, Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked up my phone and texted James, 'I think it's working.' I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly, since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing or someone rings on the door, so her nap's cut short. She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than sounds that we use to test her. We were told she had a near-normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels. I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal,\" adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life.\"\n\nA second child has also received gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only. A different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time. Up to 18 youngsters from the U.K., Spain, and the U.S. are being recruited for the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy, and \"she's the youngest globally, and that's been done to date as far as we know,\" professor Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though professor Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy who was operated on after Opal.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear So we do hope it could be a potential cure,\" he said.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the Otof gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new-era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery in September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing after four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Ms Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\n\"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years,\" Prof Bance said.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"", "source": {"uri": {"uri": "2024-05-349586843", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:26:47", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:26:47Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:01:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9254902005195618, "url": "https://www.walesonline.co.uk/news/real-life/girl-born-deaf-first-world-29134316", "title": "Girl born deaf is first to have hearing restored in new gene trial", "body": "A British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial. Opal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further. The little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear. In Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September. Her parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA. \"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times. I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon. Some 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short. She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near-normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels. I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\". A second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only. A different set of three children will get a high dose on one side.\n\nThen, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time. Up to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years. So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really. It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants. Opal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said. China has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal. Prof Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness. It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy. The treatment was currently not available on the NHS. \"What's really helped though is that the NHS does pay for genetic testing now for hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued. \"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear. And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said. \"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients. The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to {"uri": "8118182451", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:15:14", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:15:14Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:11:18Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://www.shropshirestar.com/news/uk-news/2024/05/09/british-girl-has-hearing-restored-as-first-patient-on-new-gene-therapy-trial/", "title": "British girl has hearing restored as first patient on new gene therapy trial", "body": "Opal, aged 18 months, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge and now has almost normal hearing in her right ear.\n\nA British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy. The treatment was currently not available on the NHS.\n\n\"What's really helped though is that the NHS does pay for genetic testing now for hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued.\n\n\"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\n\n\"The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to expand the range of treatments available.\"\n\nHealth and Social Care Secretary Victoria Atkins said: \"I am delighted and proud of what this government-backed trial has done for Opal, and what it could eventually do for children like her across the world.\n\n\"It marks an historic milestone as we develop a cure for this kind of deafness.\"", "source": {"uri": "shropshirestar.com", "dataType": "news", "title": {"uri": "2024-05-349577366", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:08:25", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:08:25Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:01:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://uk.news.yahoo.com/british-girl-hearing-restored-first-230100817.html", "title": "British girl has hearing restored as first patient on new gene therapy trial", "body": "A British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy. The treatment was currently not available on the NHS.\n\n\"What's really helped though is that the NHS does pay for genetic testing now for hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued.\n\n\"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\n\n\"The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to expand the range of treatments available.\"\n\nHealth and Social Care Secretary Victoria Atkins said: \"I am delighted and proud of what this government-backed trial has done for Opal, and what it could eventually do for children like her across the world.\n\n\"It marks an historic milestone as we develop a cure for this kind of deafness.\"", "source": {"uri": "uk.news.yahoo.com", "dataType": "news", "title": {"uri": "8119282273", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "14:09:02", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T14:09:02Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T14:08:37Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://www.cambridgeindependent.co.uk/news/baby-girl-born-deaf-can-now-hear-thanks-to-breakthrough-gene-9365365/", "title": "Baby girl born deaf can now hear thanks to breakthrough gene therapy at Addenbrooke's", "body": "A baby girl who was born deaf can hear unaided for the first time, thanks to a breakthrough gene therapy she received at Addenbrooke's Hospital.\n\nOpal Sandy is the first patient treated in a global trial that doctors say could lead to the dawn of a new era for gene therapies in those with hearing loss.\n\nThey described the results as \"spectacular\".\n\nOpal is the first British patient in the world and became the youngest child to receive this type of treatment when she was given it at 11 months.\n\nShe was born completely deaf due to a rare genetic condition called auditory neuropathy that is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nBut within four weeks of receiving the gene therapy infusion to her right ear, Opal responded to loud clapping - even with the cochlear implant in her left ear switched off.\n\nOpal's mother, Jo Sandy, described the results as \"mind-blowing\" and said: \"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it. I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times. I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut in the weeks that followed, clinicians noticed continuous improvement in Opal's hearing and at 24 weeks confirmed that she has close to normal hearing levels for soft sounds, such as whispering, in her treated ear.\n\n\"We were so happy when the clinical team confirmed at 24 weeks that her hearing was also picking up softer sounds and speech. Everyone was so excited such amazing results had been achieved,\" said Jo.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation.\n\n\"Since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nThe gene therapy was created by US biotech firm Regeneron.\n\nProf Manohar Bance, chief investigator of the trial, and an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, which runs Addenbrooke's, said: \"These results are spectacular and better than I expected. Gene therapy has been the future of otology and audiology for many years and I'm so excited that it is now finally here. This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by the variation of a single gene - OTOF - which produces a protein called otoferlin that is needed to allow the inner hair cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nAbout 20,000 people across the UK, Germany, France, Spain and Italy are deaf due to a mutation in the OTOF gene.\n\nChildren with a variation in this gene often pass the standard newborn screening test because the hair cells are working, but they are not communicating with the nerve.\n\nThis means their hearing loss may not be detected until they are two or three years old, by which time it will have caused a noticeable delay in their speech development.\n\nThe CHORD trial, which was launched in May 2023, was designed to show whether gene therapy can provide hearing for children born with auditory neuropathy.\n\nProf Bance said: \"We have a short time frame to intervene because of the rapid pace of brain development at this age. Delays in the diagnosis can also cause confusion for families as the many reasons for delayed speech and late intervention can impact a children's development.\"\n\nStandard NHS genetic testing can detect mutations in the OTOF gene.\n\nIn Opal's case, she was identified as being at risk as her older sister has the condition. A genetic test when she was three weeks old confirmed she had it too.\n\nIn the trial, Opal was given an infusion containing a harmless virus called AAV1, which delivered a working copy of the OTOF gene via an injection in the cochlea during surgery carried out under general anaesthesia.\n\nWhile Opal, from Oxfordshire, was being given the gene therapy in right ear during the procedure, a cochlear implant was also fitted in her left ear.\n\nJames Sandy, Opal's father said: \"It was our ultimate goal for Opal to hear all the speech sounds. It's already making a difference to our day-to-day lives, like at bath-time or swimming, when Opal can't wear her cochlear implant.\n\n\"We feel so proud to have contributed to such pivotal findings, which will hopefully help other children like Opal and their families in the future.\"\n\nOpal's 24-week results and other scientific data from the CHORD trial are being presented at the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy (ASGC) in Baltimore, USA, this week.\n\nProf Bance said: \"More than 60 years after the cochlear implant was first invented - the standard of care treatment for patients with OTOF related hearing loss - this trial shows gene therapy could provide a future alternative. It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, and positive results have recently been recorded, just six weeks after surgery.\n\nThree phases of the CHORD trial are planned, with three children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only in the first.\n\nAnother three children are due to receive a high dose in one ear in phase two. And if that too proves safe, a third phase will look at gene therapy in both ears with the dose selected after ensuring the safety and effectiveness in the first two parts.\n\nUp to 18 children from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial, with follow-up appointments continuing for five years for enrolled patients to show how patients adapt to understand speech in the longer term.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived. We've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work - to finally see something that actually worked in humans, it was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really. It felt very special.\"\n\nDr Richard Brown, consultant paediatrician at CUH, who is an investigator on the CHORD trial, said: \"The development of genomic medicine and alternative treatments is vital for patients worldwide, and increasingly offers hope to children with previously incurable disorders.\n\n\"It is likely that in the long run such treatments require less follow-up so may prove to be an attractive option, including within the developing world. Follow-up appointments have shown effective results so far with no adverse reactions and it is exciting to see the results to date.\n\n\"Within the new planned Cambridge Children's Hospital, we look forward to having a genomic centre of excellence which will support patients from across the region to access the testing they need, and the best treatment, at the right time.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy advisor at the National Deaf Children's Society, said:\n\n\"Many families will welcome these developments, and we look forward to learning about the long-term outcomes for the children treated. This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment. As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nIn Cambridge, the trial is supported by NIHR Cambridge Clinical Research Facility and NIHR Cambridge Biomedical Research Centre.\n\nProf Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\n\n\"The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to expand the range of {"uri": "8118210916", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:54:41", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:54:41Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:48:26Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8941176533699036, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/pa/article-13397961/British-girl-hearing-restored-patient-new-gene-therapy-trial.html", "title": "British girl has hearing restored as first patient on new gene...", "body": "A British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nOpal Sandy, who was born completely deaf because of a rare genetic condition (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nWhen (Opal) first turned (her head to loud clapping), I couldn't believe it. I thought it was a fluke ... but I repeated it a few times. I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked Opal's mother Jo Sandy\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy told PA.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\nOpal with her family (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\n(The trial) marks a new era in the treatment for deafness. It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions Professor Manohar Bance\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one-and-done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\nOpal is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans ... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nThe youngster is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nAs a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life Martin McLean, National Deaf Children's Society\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy. The treatment was currently not available on the NHS.\n\n\"What's really helped though is that the NHS does pay for genetic testing now for hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nHealth Secretary Victoria Atkins said she was 'delighted and proud\u00b4 of the government-backed trial (Jeff Moore/PA)\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued.\n\n\"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes,\" he said.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study, saying it would lead to learning regarding gene therapies for deafness with a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment,\" he said.\n\nThis trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients Professor Stephen Powis, NHS England\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\n\n\"The NHS has led the world in the development of a range of other innovations and therapies, and this is a very welcome addition to the work going on with life sciences organisations around England to expand the range of treatments available.\"\n\nHealth and Social Care Secretary Victoria Atkins said: \"I am delighted and proud of what this government-backed trial has done for Opal, and what it could eventually do for children like her across the world.\n\n\"It marks an historic milestone as we develop a cure for this kind of deafness.\"", "source": {"uri": "dailymail.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": "Daily Mail 'Where's daddy?' 'Who's at the door?' {"uri": "2024-05-349723919", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "03:58:59", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T03:58:59Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T18:49:52Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.886274516582489, "url": "https://www.scotsman.com/health/british-girl-has-hearing-restored-as-first-patient-on-new-gene-therapy-trial-4621290", "title": "British girl has hearing restored as first patient on new gene therapy trial", "body": "Opal, 18 months old, was born completely deaf but can now hear almost as normal and could improve further\n\nA British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, said the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James, both 33, noticed improvements to her hearing in four weeks when Opal turned her head to loud clapping.\n\n\"When she first turned, I couldn't believe it,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\"\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nBut even more impressive results were on the horizon.\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\n\"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister (Nora) waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.", "source": {"uri": {"uri": "8118917925", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "10:10:15", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T10:10:15Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T10:09:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8705882430076599, "url": "https://www.rte.ie/news/uk/2024/0509/1448171-deafness/", "title": "British girl has hearing restored after gene therapy", "body": "A British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person in the world to take part in a groundbreaking new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, aged 18 months, was born completely deaf due to the condition auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nNow, thanks to a \"one and done\" gene therapy being trialled in the UK and worldwide, Opal's hearing is almost normal - and could even improve further.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire in England, who has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital, which is part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, told the PA news agency the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and may cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\nHe said: \"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nHer parents Jo and James noticed improvements to her hearing in just four weeks, but the results were particularly impressive 24 weeks later.\n\nProf Bance said: \"In terms of being able to hear soft sounds (like a soft whisper), she can hear almost normal for her age.\n\n\"Now, we don't know that she can understand speech in the same way because she's too young, but certainly she can respond to soft sounds.\"\n\nProf Bance said Opal's hearing is now \"close to normal\", adding: \"We hope she'll get back to normal by the next testing.\"\n\nHe added that the treatment is \"a one and done therapy, so hopefully you have your treatment and then you go back to your life\".\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed-up for five years.\n\nRegarding the success of Opal's case, Prof Bance said: \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away', and I've been in practice about 30 years.\n\n\"So, for me, it was almost unreal that this moment had arrived.\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work.. to finally see something that actually worked in humans....It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really.\n\n\"It felt very special.\"\n\nAt the moment, the gold standard treatment for auditory neuropathy is cochlear implants.\n\nOpal had one fitted to her left ear at the same time as she underwent gene therapy in her right ear, to ensure she got hearing as soon as possible.\n\nHowever, according to Prof Bance, implants can have limitations such as difficulty filtering out background noise when multiple people are speaking.\n\nAs a result, scientists have been working on experimental gene therapies to advance the field.\n\nOpal is the first patient globally to receive the Regeneron therapy and \"she's the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know,\" Prof Bance said.\n\nChina has also been working on targeting the same gene, with positive results, though Prof Bance said theirs uses a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy, who was operated on after Opal.\n\nProf Bance said he believes the trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", adding: \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\n\n\"It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic-related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nHe said it could take a while for more children to benefit from gene therapy, depending on how quickly the trial can enrol patients, costs to the NHS and whether it can be scaled up.\n\nOpal's surgery, which was carried out under general anaesthetic, was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, Prof Bance continued.\n\n\"So basically, we find the inner ear and we open the inner ear and we infuse the treatment, in this particular case using a catheter, over 16 minutes.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nThe gene therapy used in the Chord trial is called DB-OTO and is specifically for children with OTOF mutations.\n\nA modified and harmless virus is used to carry the working gene, enabling genetic material to be delivered to specific cells to restore hearing function.\n\nProf Bance estimates that around 20,000 people in the US, Germany, France, Italy, Spain and the UK have auditory neuropathy due to OTOF mutations.\n\nChildren with a variation in the OTOF gene are born with severe to profound hearing loss, but they often pass the newborn hearing screening tests and people think they can hear.\n\nMartin McLean, senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, said: \"Many families will welcome these developments, and we look forward to learning about the long-term outcomes for the children treated.\n\n\"This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment.\n\n\"As a charity, we support families to make informed choices about medical technologies, so that they can give their deaf child the best possible start in life.\"\n\nResults from the Chord study were presented yesterday to the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy conference in Baltimore in the US.", "source": {"uri": "rte.ie", "dataType": "news", "title": "RTE.ie"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri":
