So we do hope it could be a potential cure\".\n\nHe said it came on the back of decades of work and marked \"a new era in the treatment of deafness\".\n\nWhat genetic defect caused hearing loss?\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire in south central England, has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nHow was it rectified?\n\nThe therapy uses a modified, harmless virus to deliver a working copy of the Otof gene into these cells. Given as an infusion into the ear, the therapy replaces faulty DNA causing her type of inherited deafness.\n\nOpal had the therapy in her right ear, under general anaesthetic, and a cochlear implant put into her left.\n\nHow gene therapy works? First, DNA is added to a harmless virus to create the gene therapy. Then, the gene therapy is introduced into the inner ear to repair the damaged hair cells, enabling the child to hear.\n\nJust a few weeks later, she could hear loud sounds, such as clapping, in her right ear. And after six months, her doctors, at Addenbrooke's Hospital, in Cambridge, confirmed that ear had almost normal hearing for soft sounds - even very quiet whispers.\n\nBance said that following surgery last September, Opal's hearing was now \"close to normal\" with further improvement expected.\n\nA second child received the gene therapy in Cambridge with positive results seen six weeks after the surgery.\n\nWhat percentage of hearing loss in babies is due to genetic cause?\n\nExperts hope the therapy could also work for other types of profound hearing loss. Up to 60% of hearing-loss cases in children have a genetic cause.\n\n3-stage trial The trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the United States are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nAnd Prof Bance hopes the trial can lead to gene therapy being used for more common types of hearing loss.\n\n\"What I am hoping is that we can start to use gene therapy in young children... where we actually restore the hearing and they don't have to have cochlear implants and other technologies that have to be replaced,\" he told BBC.\n\nWhat's the current treatment for auditory neuropathy?\n\nChina has been working on targeting the same gene though Bance said theirs used a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy.\n\nOpal was the first person to take part in a gene therapy trial being carried out in Cambridge by Bance. \"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away'... to finally see something that actually worked in humans... {"uri": "8118764355", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "08:32:49", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T08:32:49Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T08:29:42Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8549019694328308, "url": "https://www.yahoo.com/news/deaf-toddler-hear-gene-therapy-050300058.html", "title": "Deaf toddler can hear after gene therapy world first", "body": "A deaf toddler is now able to hear after becoming the first patient in the world to receive a new gene therapy.\n\nOpal Sandy, an 18-month-old girl from Oxfordshire, was born with a genetic form of a condition called auditory neuropathy, which disrupts nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain and prevents sound being processed.\n\nIn September, she became the first person in the world to receive gene replacement treatment as part of a global trial, and experts are excited by the \"potential cure\" for the condition.\n\nOpal, like her five-year-old sister Nora, was born with a faulty version of the OTOF gene, which carries the instructions for a neurotransmitter to be produced that allows the ear to communicate with the brain. The malfunction causes severe hearing loss.\n\nNora's diagnosis meant Opal was eligible for genetic testing on the NHS, which detected she also had the faulty gene. The condition had not been picked up by newborn screening tests in either child.\n\nWhile Nora has cochlear implants in both ears, which are the current gold-standard treatment, Opal was able to join the gene therapy trial, although she also has an implant in one ear as a safety measure.\n\nThe gene therapy, called DB-OTO and manufactured by Regeneron, works by replacing the faulty gene with a working version. It is administered by a single injection of a modified and harmless virus carrying the new genetic material.\n\nThe treatment means the ear's sensory cells are able to produce a vital protein called otoferlin that is required for the tiny hair cells in the ear to transmit signals to the brain.\n\nOpal's parents, Jo and James Sandy, noticed improvements after just three weeks and things have only improved since. Opal's doctors say her hearing is now \"almost normal for her age\" although progress will become clearer as she learns to talk.\n\nMs Sandy, a 33-year-old geography teacher, said it was \"bonkers\" how quickly her daughter became able to hear.\n\nShe videoed her daughter responding to the sound for the first time.\n\nThe parents had become accustom to trying to get Opal's attention by making loud noise from behind her back but to little avail.\n\nUntil one day she turned to the sound of her mother clapping as she played.\n\n\"We were in the routine of testing quite loud sounds like banging, clapping, wooden spoons on saucepans, that kind of really intermittent loud noise,\" she said.\n\n\"I was testing that with her implant on and hadn't realised that her implant had actually come off, and she turned to pretty loud clapping. When she first turned, I couldn't believe it.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times. I was absolutely gobsmacked.\"\n\nOpal, who had her surgery at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, is one of up to 18 children due to be enrolled on the trial, which is taking place in the UK, US and Spain.\n\nWhile Opal received a low dose to one ear, others will be given higher doses to the other ear, before some children receive the treatment in both ears once there is more evidence it is safe.\n\nProf Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, which runs Addebnbrooke's, and the trial's chief investigator, said the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" after 24 weeks.\n\n\"We have results from [Opal] which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\n\n\"In terms of being able to hear soft sounds (like a soft whisper), she can hear almost normal for her age,\" he said. \"We don't know that she can understand speech in the same way because she's too young, but certainly she can respond to soft sounds.\"\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery, while other gene therapies for deafness are being trialled in China and the US.\n\nProf Bance estimates that around 20,000 people in the US, Germany, France, Italy, Spain and the UK have auditory neuropathy due to OTOF mutations.\n\nMartin McLean, the senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, said: \"This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment.\"\n\nMrs Sandy said Opal had started to talk in the last six weeks and now both her daughters like \"seeing who can make the most noise\".\n\n\"She's good at all your common first baby words, so 'daddy' is a favourite, 'uh oh', 'bye bye',\" she said.\n\n\"I always said I'd never get annoyed with them making noise and I do get annoyed with them making noise,\" Mrs Sandy joked.\n\n\"Opal loves playing with her little musical instrument set ... playing the drums, playing her little piano, tapping some of her wooden blocks and things like that.\n\n\"Nora got into music quite recently and [Opal] likes to put her arms up and does little dances in the kitchen.\n\n\"So they like dancing together. Nora likes reading to her, they like fighting, they like jumping off the sofa.\"\n\nThe results from the Chord trial were presented on Wednesday to the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy The surgeon and chief investigator for the trial said he hoped she would be the first of many people to benefit from new kinds of gene therapies that can treat inner ear issues, such as hers, and potentially other kinds of hearing loss as well further down the line.\n\n\"These results are spectacular and better than I expected,\" said Professor Manohar Bance, a surgeon and chief investigator for the trial.\n\n\"Gene therapy has been the future of otology and audiology for many years and I'm so excited that it is now finally here. This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nHow does the treatment work?\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy - from biotech firm Regeneron - delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nIn Opal's case, she received an infusion containing the working gene to her right ear during surgery last September.\n\nOpal's surgery was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, according to Professor Bance.\n\nHe said the inner ear (cochlea) was opened and the treatment infused using a catheter over 16 minutes.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear,\" he said.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nCan anyone who is deaf benefit?\n\nChildren with a variation in the OTOF gene often pass the newborn screening, as the hair cells are working, but they are not talking to the nerve.\n\nIt means this hearing loss is not commonly detected until children are 2 or 3 years of age - when a delay in speech is likely to be noticed.\n\nProfessor Bance said: \"We have a short time frame to intervene because of the rapid pace of brain development at this age. Delays in the diagnosis can also cause confusion for families as the many reasons for delayed speech and late intervention can impact children's development.\"\n\nAbout 20,000 people across the US, the UK, Germany, France, Spain and Italy are thought to have auditory neuropathy due to OTOF mutations.\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery.\n\nThe overall phase 1/2 Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nProfessor Stephen Powis, NHS England's national medical director, said: \"This trial will transform the life of 18-month-old Opal Sandy while offering hope to many others like her, and is another example of the NHS being a global leader in developing gene therapy for patients.\"\n\nMartin McLean, of the National Deaf Children's Society, welcomed the study and said: \"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment.\"", "source": {"uri": "inews.co.uk", "dataType": "news", {"uri": "8118769842", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "08:35:29", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T08:35:29Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T08:33:43Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://www.miragenews.com/baby-born-deaf-can-hear-after-breakthrough-gene-1231508/", "title": "Baby Born Deaf Can Hear After Breakthrough Gene Therapy", "body": "A baby girl born deaf can hear unaided for the first time, after receiving gene therapy when she was eleven months old at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge.\n\nGene therapy has been the future of otology and audiology for many years and I'm so excited that it is now finally here\n\nManohar Bance\n\nOpal Sandy from Oxfordshire is the first patient treated in a global gene therapy trial, which shows \"mind-blowing\" results. She is the first British patient in the world and the youngest child to receive this type of treatment.\n\nOpal was born completely deaf because of a rare genetic condition, auditory neuropathy, caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nWithin four weeks of having the gene therapy infusion to her right ear, Opal responded to sound, even with the cochlear implant in her left ear switched off.\n\nClinicians noticed continuous improvement in Opal's hearing in the weeks afterwards. At 24 weeks, they confirmed Opal had close to normal hearing levels for soft sounds, such as whispering, in her treated ear.\n\nNow 18 months old, Opal can respond to her parents' voices and can communicate words such as \"Dada\" and \"bye-bye.\"\n\nOpal's mother, Jo Sandy, said: \"When Opal could first hear us clapping unaided it was mind-blowing - we were so happy when the clinical team confirmed at 24 weeks that her hearing was also picking up softer sounds and speech. The phrase 'near normal' hearing was used and everyone was so excited such amazing results had been achieved.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be due to a variation in a single gene, known as the OTOF gene. The gene produces a protein called otoferlin, needed to allow the inner hair cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve. Approximately 20,000 people across the UK, Germany, France, Spain, Italy and UK and are deaf due to a mutation in the OTOF gene.\n\nThe CHORD trial, which started in May 2023, aims to show whether gene therapy can provide hearing for children born with auditory neuropathy.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance from the Department of Clinical Neurosciences at the University of Cambridge and an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust is chief investigator of the trial. He said:\n\n\"These results are spectacular and better than I expected. Gene therapy has been the future of otology and audiology for many years and I'm so excited that it is now finally here. This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss.\"\n\nChildren with a variation in the OTOF gene often pass the newborn screening, as the hair cells are working, but they are not talking to the nerve. It means this hearing loss is not commonly detected until children are 2 or 3 years of age - when a delay in speech is likely to be noticed.\n\nProfessor Bance added: \"We have a short time frame to intervene because of the rapid pace of brain development at this age. Delays in the diagnosis can also cause confusion for families as the many reasons for delayed speech and late intervention can impact a children's development.\"\n\n\"More than sixty years after the cochlear implant was first invented - the standard of care treatment for patients with OTOF related hearing loss - this trial shows gene therapy could provide a future alternative. It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness. It also supports the development of other gene therapies that may prove to make a difference in other genetic related hearing conditions, many of which are more common than auditory neuropathy.\"\n\nMutations in the OTOF gene can be identified by standard NHS genetic testing. Opal was identified as being at risk as her older sister has the condition; this was confirmed by genetic test result when she was 3 weeks old.\n\nOpal was given an infusion containing a harmless virus (AAV1). It delivers a working copy of the OTOF gene and is delivered via an injection in the cochlea during surgery under general anaesthesia. During surgery, while Opal was given the gene therapy in right ear, a cochlear implant was fitted in her left ear.\n\nJames Sandy, Opal's father said: \"It was our ultimate goal for Opal to hear all the speech sounds. It's already making a difference to our day-to-day lives, like at bath-time or swimming, when Opal can't wear her cochlear implant. We feel so proud to have contributed to such pivotal findings, which will hopefully help other children like Opal and their families in the future.\"\n\nOpal's 24-week results, alongside other scientific data from the CHORD trial are being presented at the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy (ASGC) in Baltimore, USA this week.\n\nDr Richard Brown, Consultant Paediatrician at CUH, who is an Investigator on the CHORD trial, said: \"The development of genomic medicine and alternative treatments is vital for patients worldwide, and increasingly offers hope to children with previously incurable disorders. It is likely that in the long run such treatments require less follow up so may prove to be an attractive option, including within the developing world. Follow up appointments have shown effective results so far with no adverse reactions and it is exciting to see the results to date.\n\n\"Within the new planned Cambridge Children's Hospital, we look forward to having a genomic centre of excellence which will support patients from across the region to access the testing they need, and the best treatment, at the right time.\"\n\nThe CHORD trial has been funded by Regeneron. Patients are being enrolled in the study in the US, UK and Spain.\n\nPatients in the first phase of the study receive a low dose to one ear. The second phase are expected to use a higher dose of gene therapy in one ear only, following proven safety of the starting dose. The third phase will look at gene therapy in both ears with the dose selected after ensuring the safety and effectiveness in parts 1 and 2. Follow up appointments will continue for five years for enrolled patients, which will show how patients adapt to understand speech in the longer term.\n\nIn Cambridge, the trial is supported by NIHR Cambridge Clinical Research Facility and NIHR Cambridge Biomedical Research Centre.\n\nAdapted from a press release from CUH\n\n/University Release. So we do hope it could be a potential cure'.\n\nHe said it came on the back of decades of work and marked 'a new era in the treatment of deafness'.\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire in south central England, has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the 'new era' gene therapy from Regeneron delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nBance said that following surgery last September, Opal's hearing was now 'close to normal' with further improvement expected.\n\nA second child received the gene therapy in Cambridge with positive results seen six weeks after the surgery.\n\nChina has been working on targeting the same gene though Bance said theirs used a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy.\n\nOpal was the first person to take part in a gene therapy trial being carried out in Cambridge by Bance.\n\nThe trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the United States are being recruited to the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nBance said the current treatment for auditory neuropathy was implants.\n\n'My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away' to finally see something that {"uri": "2024-05-349773718", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "05:12:53", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T05:12:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T05:03:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8313725590705872, "url": "https://uk.news.yahoo.com/deaf-toddler-hear-gene-therapy-050300058.html", "title": "Deaf toddler can hear after gene therapy world first", "body": "Opal Sandy, an 18-month-old girl from Oxfordshire, was born with a genetic form of a condition called auditory neuropathy, which disrupts nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain and prevents sound being processed.\n\nIn September, she became the first person in the world to receive gene replacement treatment as part of a global trial, and experts are excited by the \"potential cure\" for the condition.\n\nOpal, like her five-year-old sister Nora, was born with a faulty version of the OTOF gene, which carries the instructions for a neurotransmitter to be produced that allows the ear to communicate with the brain. The malfunction causes severe hearing loss.\n\nNora's diagnosis meant Opal was eligible for genetic testing on the NHS, which detected she also had the faulty gene.The condition had not been picked up by newborn screening tests in either child.\n\nWhile Nora has cochlear implants in both ears, which are the current gold-standard treatment, Opal was able to join the gene therapy trial, although she also has an implant in one ear as a safety measure.\n\nThe gene therapy, called DB-OTO and manufactured by Regeneron, works by replacing the faulty gene with a working version. It is administered by a single injection of a modified and harmless virus carrying the new genetic material.\n\nThe treatment means the ear's sensory cells are able to produce a vital protein called otoferlin that is required for the tiny hair cells in the ear to transmit signals to the brain.\n\nOpal's parents, Jo and James Sandy, noticed improvements after just three weeks and things have only improved since. Opal's doctors say her hearing is now \"almost normal for her age\" although progress will become clearer as she learns to talk.\n\nJo Sandy, a 33-year-old geography teacher, said it was \"bonkers\" how quickly her daughter became able to hear.\n\n\"We were in the routine of testing quite loud sounds like banging, clapping, wooden spoons on saucepans, that kind of really intermittent loud noise,\" she said.\n\n\"I was testing that with her implant on and hadn't realised that her implant had actually come off, and she turned to pretty loud clapping. When she first turned, I couldn't believe it.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times. I was absolutely gobsmacked.\"\n\nOpal, who had her surgery at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, is one of up to 18 children due to be enrolled on the trial, which is taking place in the UK, US and Spain.\n\nWhile Opal received a low dose to one ear, others will be given higher doses to the other ear, before some children receive the treatment in both ears once there is more evidence it is safe.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, which runs Addebnbrooke's, and the trial's chief investigator, said the results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" after 24 weeks.\n\n'We hope it could be a potential cure'\n\n\"We have results from [Opal] which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\n\n\"In terms of being able to hear soft sounds (like a soft whisper), she can hear almost normal for her age,\" he said. \"We don't know that she can understand speech in the same way because she's too young, but certainly she can respond to soft sounds.\"\n\nA second child has also received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge University Hospitals, with positive results seen recently, six weeks after surgery, while other gene therapies for deafness are being trialled in China and the US.\n\nProf Bance estimates that around 20,000 people in the US, Germany, France, Italy, Spain and the UK have auditory neuropathy due to OTOF mutations.\n\nMartin McLean, the senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, said: \"This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\n\n\"We would like to emphasise that, with the right support from the start, deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment.\"\n\nSisters 'compete to make most noise'\n\nMrs Sandy said Opal had started to talk in the last six weeks and now both her daughters like \"seeing who can make the most noise\".\n\n\"She's good at all your common first baby words, so 'daddy' is a favourite, 'uh oh', 'bye bye',\" she said.\n\n\"I always said I'd never get annoyed with them making noise and I do get annoyed with them making noise,\" Mrs Sandy joked.\n\n\"Opal loves playing with her little musical instrument set ... playing the drums, playing her little piano, tapping some of her wooden blocks and things like that.\n\n\"Nora got into music quite recently and (Opal) likes to put her arms up and does little dances in the kitchen.\n\n\"So they like dancing together. Nora likes reading to her, they like fighting, they like jumping off the sofa.\"\n\nThe results from the Chord trial were presented on Wednesday to the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy {"uri": "8119843461", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "21:58:38", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T21:58:38Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T21:57:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://www.sunjournal.com/2024/05/09/deaf-baby-hears-for-the-first-time-after-groundbreaking-gene-therapy-trial/", "title": "Deaf baby hears for the first time after 'groundbreaking' gene therapy trial", "body": "LONDON -- A baby girl born with profound genetic deafness can now hear unaided after receiving a \"groundbreaking\" gene therapy trial, Britain's National Health Service said Thursday.\n\nOpal Sandy, an 18-month-old from Oxfordshire, England, is the first patient treated in a global gene therapy trial that is showing \"mind-blowing\" results, Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge said in a statement. Opal is \"the first British patient in the world and the youngest child to receive this type of treatment,\" the hospital said.\n\nThe treatment is already having practical results, the hospital said: Opal can now respond to her parents' voices and can communicate words such as \"Dada\" and \"bye-bye.\"\n\n\"When Opal could first hear us clapping unaided it was mind-blowing,\" her mother, Jo Sandy, said in a statement. \"We were so happy when the clinical team confirmed at 24 weeks that her hearing was also picking up softer sounds and speech.\"\n\nHer father, James Sandy, said that it was \"already making a difference to our day-to-day lives, like at bath-time or swimming. ... We feel so proud to have contributed to such pivotal findings, which will hopefully help other children like Opal and their families in the future.\"\n\nThe results are another example of the potential of gene therapies, which use the insertion of genetic material to treat diseases.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nOpal, like her elder sister, was born deaf because of a rare genetic condition known as auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses traveling from the inner ear to the brain, the hospital said. However, within four weeks of having the gene therapy infusion to her right ear, Opal \"responded to sound,\" it added. Now, at 24 weeks after treatment, clinicians have been able to confirm \"close to normal hearing levels for soft sounds,\" even including \"whispering, in her treated ear.\"\n\nManohar Bance, chief investigator of the trial and an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, said the results were \"spectacular and better than I expected.\"\n\n\"This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be due to a variation in a gene, known as the OTOF gene, which produces a protein needed to allow the inner hair cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve. According to the NHS, about 20,000 people across Britain, Germany, France, Spain and Italy are deaf because of a mutation in the OTOF gene.\n\nThis type of hearing loss is not commonly detected until children are about 2 or 3 years of age, when a delay in speech is likely to be noticed, the Cambridge hospital said. However, Opal's was detected earlier, when she was just 3 weeks old, as her older sister also has the same genetic condition.\n\nThe trial that Opal was a part of began in May last year and was sponsored by U.S. biotechnology company Regeneron. It is continuing to enroll patients in the study in the U.S., Britain and Spain, it said.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe procedure involved Opal being given an infusion via an injection into her right cochlea during surgery, containing a modified virus known as AAV1, which acts as a \"working copy of the OTOF gene.\" A cochlear implant was also fitted at the same time to her left ear.\n\nCongenital deafness - hearing loss present at birth - is a \"significant unmet medical need that affects approximately 1.7 out of every 1,000 children born\" in the United States, according to Regeneron.\n\n\"While hearing aids and cochlear implants can amplify sound to improve hearing for individuals with a range of hearing loss, these devices do not currently restore the full spectrum of sound,\" it added.\n\nThe news has been welcomed in Britain, making national headlines, but it could have a global impact.\n\n\"Many families will welcome these developments, and we look forward to learning about the long-term outcomes for the children treated,\" Martin McLean of Britain's National Deaf Children's Society said in an emailed statement Thursday. \"This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\"\n\nThe trial was expected to include up to 18 children from Britain, the U.S. and Spain, the hospital said in a statement when the trial began last year. Their progress will be followed for up to five years to see the extent to which their hearing improves.\n\nOther clinical trials looking into gene therapies as a way to restore hearing in children with deafness caused by OTOF gene variations are also underway elsewhere in the world. Last year, an 11-year-old boy, Aissam Dam, received treatment in a separate gene therapy trial at the Children's Hospital of Philadelphia. China is also conducting a number of trials.\n\nOpal's tentatively successful results at 24 weeks after treatment, along with other scientific data from the trial, are set to be presented at the annual meeting of the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy in Baltimore this week.\n\nCopy the Story Link\n\nInvalid username/password.\n\nPlease check your email to confirm and complete your registration.\n\nCreate a commenting profile by providing an email address, password and display name. {"uri": "8118191478", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:23:27", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:23:27Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:22:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://news.sky.com/story/british-girls-hearing-restored-in-pioneering-gene-therapy-trial-13131548", "title": "British girl's hearing restored in pioneering gene therapy trial", "body": "Opal's mum says she was \"gobsmacked\" when she realised her daughter could hear without her cochlear implant - and experts say the results are better than they expected.\n\nA British girl has had her hearing restored after becoming the first person worldwide to take part in a pioneering new gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy was born totally deaf due to auditory neuropathy, which disrupts nerve impulses from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nBut the 18-month-old's hearing is now almost normal - and could improve further - after having the one-time treatment.\n\nOpal was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge and the head of the trial, Professor Manohar Bance, said results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and he hopes medics might be able to cure others with this type of deafness.\n\n\"We have results from (Opal) which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure,\" he said.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which makes a protein called otoferlin and enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nOpal, from Oxfordshire, had an infusion of the working gene into her right ear during surgery in September - the treatment developed by biotech firm Regeneron.\n\nHer parents noticed changes in only four weeks and the improvements were especially noticeable 24 weeks later.\n\nJo and James Sandy, both 33, said they were \"gobsmacked\" when she responded to sound tests at home without her cochlear implant, the usual way to treat the condition.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times,\" said Mrs Sandy.\n\nThey say she now enjoys the sound of slamming her cutlery on the table and playing with toy drums and wooden blocks.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time,\" she added. \"I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nOpal's surgery was very similar to fitting a cochlear implant, according to Prof Bance.\n\nHe said the inner ear (cochlea) was opened and the treatment infused using a catheter over 16 minutes.\n\n\"We have to make a release hole in another part of the ear to let the treatment out because it has to go all the way through the ear,\" he said.\n\n\"And then we just repair and close up, so it's actually a very similar approach to a cochlear implant, except we don't put the implant in.\"\n\nRead more from Sky News:\n\nSperm whale 'phonetic alphabet' discovered\n\nOrangutan uses plant to treat wound\n\nHe said the gene therapy potentially \"marks a new era in the treatment for deafness\".\n\n\"It was just the fact that we've been hearing about this for so long, and there's been so much work, decades of work... to finally see something that actually worked in humans... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really,\" he said.\n\nThe treatment was developed especially for children with OTOF mutations, and a second child who has had the same surgery is also seeing positive results.\n\nThe trial has three parts - with three deaf children, including Opal, getting a low dose in just one ear.\n\nAnother three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if safe, a new set of children will get a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 people from the UK, US and Spain are being recruited and will be followed up for five years.\n\nResults In a groundbreaking gene therapy trial, she has now regained her hearing. The trial involved injecting a harmless virus containing the correct gene into her ear.\n\nA deaf toddler has had her hearing restored after being the first patient treated in a gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, who is 18 months old, has auditory neuropathy, a condition caused by a faulty gene.\n\nThe condition means the ear can detect sound but doesn't transmit it to the brain properly. It can cause degrees of hearing loss, but in Opal's case, she was fully deaf from birth.\n\nNow Opal, who lives in Oxfordshire in England, can hear almost normally thanks to a 16-minute long gene therapy procedure.\n\nDoctors injected Opal's right ear with a harmless virus that carried a working version of the faulty gene while she was under general anesthesia.\n\nWithin four weeks of receiving the gene therapy, Opal could respond to sound. After 24 weeks, she had hearing levels that were \"close to normal,\" according to a press release from Britain's health service.\n\nShe can hear whispering in her treated ear, respond to her parents' voices, and has even begun saying words such as \"daddy\" and \"bye-bye\" herself.\n\nAround 1 in 500 babies are born with hearing loss or develop it in early childhood, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention in the US. 50 to 60% of this hearing loss is due to genetic causes.\n\nThe World Health Organization estimates that unaddressed hearing loss represents a global cost of $980 billion a year in costs such as healthcare and educational support.\n\nThe \"start of a new era\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy is caused by a faulty gene and isn't usually detected until a child is 2 to 3 years old, when delays in speech are more noticeable. But Opal's was picked up early through gene testing, which she was eligible for because her older sister has the condition.\n\nUsually, auditory neuropathy is treated with implants, but Opal was put on a trial at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, England, which is trialing gene therapy on children.\n\nHer treatment was part of phase one of the trial, where children are given a low dose in one ear. Phase two will involve a higher dose, still in one ear, and phase three will move on to treating both ears at a dose deemed safe from the previous phases.\n\nOpal was the first patient, treated in September 2023 when she was 11 months old. A second child received the therapy and has seen good results six weeks post-treatment, according to a release from Regeneron, the pharmaceutical company that makes the infusion.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon and chief investigator of the trial, said the results were \"spectacular and better than I expected.\"\n\n\"This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the Sandy, a 33-year-old secondary school geography teacher, and her husband James, 33, who works in car manufacturing, said they were \"gobsmacked\" when they realised Opal could hear without the need for a cochlear implant.\n\nOpal now loves slamming her cutlery on the table to make a noise and enjoys playing with toy drums, a piano and wooden blocks.\n\nThe family, from Oxfordshire, have an older daughter Nora, aged five, who has the same genetic form of auditory neuropathy as Opal and wears cochlear implants, which is the current gold standard treatment.\n\nOpal Sandy, who was born completely deaf because of a rare genetic condition (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\nMrs Sandy told the PA news agency the couple found out that Nora was deaf when she was nine months old and medics said any subsequent children would be entitled to additional hearing tests at birth.\n\n\"Although Nora and Opal passed the newborn hearing screening, which picks up the majority of deafness, when Opal was a newborn she went for the additional testing and we found out she was deaf when she was four days old,\" she said.\n\n\"Nora had her cochlear implants on both sides fitted at 15 months old and following that comes quite an intense rehabilitation process of speech and language therapy and audiologist appointments.\n\n\"She's done really well learning to speak and has managed to close the language gap with her peers.\n\n\"So, hearing that Opal was deaf - of course there was a grieving process that we went through the same as when we found out that Nora was as well - but Nora had set the bar really high and we knew what was possible with lots of hard work and support from lots of people.\"\n\nThe couple first heard about the CHORD gene therapy trial from the ear, nose and throat surgeon at their local John Radcliffe Hospital in Oxford.\n\nThe surgeon knew of Professor Manohar Bance's work and that he was running a trial using a gene therapy from biotech firm Regeneron at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge.\n\nOpal Sandy, who was born completely deaf because of a rare genetic condition (Andrew Matthews/PA)\n\n\"Our initial reaction was we were really nervous,\" Mrs Sandy said, adding it sounded \"too good to be true\".\n\nShe said: \"We were quite nervous to go down a different path to one that we knew had already worked so well for our eldest daughter. But it also sounded like a really unique opportunity.\n\n\"We sort of had a relatively reliable safety net that, even if it didn't work, Opal would still be eligible to have a cochlear implant in her other ear six months down the line.\n\n\"So that was like a really good sort of safety blanket that we could fall back on if we needed to.\"\n\nOpal received a gene therapy infusion in her right ear during surgery last September. At the same time, a cochlear implant was fitted in her left ear to ensure she had hearing.\n\nThere was then a tense wait to see if the therapy had worked, but it became apparent quite quickly that Opal could hear.\n\n\"It was about three weeks post-surgery, which was about a week after her implant had been turned on,\" Mrs Sandy explained.\n\n\"So we were sort of in the routine of testing quite loud sounds like banging, clapping, wooden spoons on saucepans, that kind of really intermittent loud noise.\n\n\"I was testing that with her implant on and hadn't realised that her implant had actually come off, and she turned to pretty loud clapping. When she first turned, I couldn't believe it.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nThe couple had been told they might notice a change within the first six weeks, hence Mrs Sandy's surprise.\n\n\"I couldn't really believe it,\" she said. \"It was ... bonkers.\"\n\nShe added that \"there is no way in a million years I thought that Opal would be able to turn to sound without wearing an implant\".\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\nNow, even without the implant in her left ear, Opal can hear perfectly well thanks to the gene therapy.\n\nMrs Sandy said: \"Without an implant, she can understand basically the same things that she can understand when it's on, so 'Opal, where's your nose? 'Where's daddy?' 'Who's at the door?' 'Bye bye' ...sort of basic language acquisition, that she can understand just as well with her implant on and off.\"\n\nMr Sandy said he noticed a \"massive\" improvement in the 18 to 24 weeks post-surgery and the \"big moment\" was hearing from the team at Cambridge University Hospitals that Opal had near-normal hearing at 24 weeks.\n\nMrs Sandy added: \"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nOpal has also started speaking in the last six weeks.\n\n\"She's good at all your common first baby words, so 'daddy' is a favourite, 'uh oh', 'bye bye',\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\nAs for playing, Mrs Sandy said both Nora and Opal like \"seeing who can make the most noise\".\n\nShe joked: \"I always said I'd never get annoyed with them making noise and I do get annoyed with them making noise.\n\n\"So, Opal loves playing with her little musical instrument set ... playing the drums, playing her little piano, tapping some of her wooden blocks and things like that.\n\n\"She's started becoming more interested in books, so like lots of lift the flap books, 'where's Spot?'...those kinds of really interactive books, she really likes.\n\n\"She loves slamming her cutlery on the table asking us where her dinner is.\n\n\"Nora got into music quite recently and (Opal) likes put her arms up and does little dances in the kitchen.\n\n\"So they like dancing together. {"uri": "8119821751", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "21:33:53", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T21:33:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T21:32:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8117647171020508, "url": "https://www.pressherald.com/2024/05/09/deaf-baby-hears-for-the-first-time-after-groundbreaking-gene-therapy-trial/", "title": "Deaf baby hears for the first time after 'groundbreaking' gene therapy trial", "body": "LONDON -- A baby girl born with profound genetic deafness can now hear unaided after receiving a \"groundbreaking\" gene therapy trial, Britain's National Health Service said Thursday.\n\nOpal Sandy, an 18-month-old from Oxfordshire, England, is the first patient treated in a global gene therapy trial that is showing \"mind-blowing\" results, Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge said in a statement. Opal is \"the first British patient in the world and the youngest child to receive this type of treatment,\" the hospital said.\n\nThe treatment is already having practical results, the hospital said: Opal can now respond to her parents' voices and can communicate words such as \"Dada\" and \"bye-bye.\"\n\n\"When Opal could first hear us clapping unaided it was mind-blowing,\" her mother, Jo Sandy, said in a statement. \"We were so happy when the clinical team confirmed at 24 weeks that her hearing was also picking up softer sounds and speech.\"\n\nHer father, James Sandy, said that it was \"already making a difference to our day-to-day lives, like at bath-time or swimming. ... We feel so proud to have contributed to such pivotal findings, which will hopefully help other children like Opal and their families in the future.\"\n\nThe results are another example of the potential of gene therapies, which use the insertion of genetic material to treat diseases.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nOpal, like her elder sister, was born deaf because of a rare genetic condition known as auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses traveling from the inner ear to the brain, the hospital said. However, within four weeks of having the gene therapy infusion to her right ear, Opal \"responded to sound,\" it added. Now, at 24 weeks after treatment, clinicians have been able to confirm \"close to normal hearing levels for soft sounds,\" even including \"whispering, in her treated ear.\"\n\nManohar Bance, chief investigator of the trial and an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, said the results were \"spectacular and better than I expected.\"\n\n\"This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss,\" he said.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be due to a variation in a gene, known as the OTOF gene, which produces a protein needed to allow the inner hair cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve. According to the NHS, about 20,000 people across Britain, Germany, France, Spain and Italy are deaf because of a mutation in the OTOF gene.\n\nThis type of hearing loss is not commonly detected until children are about 2 or 3 years of age, when a delay in speech is likely to be noticed, the Cambridge hospital said. However, Opal's was detected earlier, when she was just 3 weeks old, as her older sister also has the same genetic condition.\n\nThe trial that Opal was a part of began in May last year and was sponsored by U.S. biotechnology company Regeneron. It is continuing to enroll patients in the study in the U.S., Britain and Spain, it said.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe procedure involved Opal being given an infusion via an injection into her right cochlea during surgery, containing a modified virus known as AAV1, which acts as a \"working copy of the OTOF gene.\" A cochlear implant was also fitted at the same time to her left ear.\n\nCongenital deafness - hearing loss present at birth - is a \"significant unmet medical need that affects approximately 1.7 out of every 1,000 children born\" in the United States, according to Regeneron.\n\n\"While hearing aids and cochlear implants can amplify sound to improve hearing for individuals with a range of hearing loss, these devices do not currently restore the full spectrum of sound,\" it added.\n\nThe news has been welcomed in Britain, making national headlines, but it could have a global impact.\n\n\"Many families will welcome these developments, and we look forward to learning about the long-term outcomes for the children treated,\" Martin McLean of Britain's National Deaf Children's Society said in an emailed statement Thursday. \"This trial will teach us more about the effectiveness of gene therapy in those cases where deafness has a specific genetic cause.\"\n\nThe trial was expected to include up to 18 children from Britain, the U.S. and Spain, the hospital said in a statement when the trial began last year. Their progress will be followed for up to five years to see the extent to which their hearing improves.\n\nOther clinical trials looking into gene therapies as a way to restore hearing in children with deafness caused by OTOF gene variations are also underway elsewhere in the world. Last year, an 11-year-old boy, Aissam Dam, received treatment in a separate gene therapy trial at the Children's Hospital of Philadelphia. China is also conducting a number of trials.\n\nOpal's tentatively successful results at 24 weeks after treatment, along with other scientific data from the trial, are set to be presented at the annual meeting of the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy in Baltimore this week.\n\nCopy the Story Link\n\nInvalid username/password.\n\nPlease check your email to confirm and complete your registration.\n\nCreate a commenting profile by providing an email address, password and display name. {"uri": "8119175113", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "12:58:55", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T12:58:55Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T12:58:40Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8117647171020508, "url": "https://www.ndtv.com/world-news/1-year-old-uk-girls-hearing-restored-after-groundbreaking-gene-therapy-5625908", "title": "1-Year-Old UK Girl's Hearing Restored After Groundbreaking Gene Therapy", "body": "Several medical teams around the world including in China and the United States have been trialling similar treatments with good results for hereditary deafness that focuses on a rare genetic mutation.\n\nThe girl has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy. (Representational)\n\nLondon:\n\nAn 18-month-old British girl who was born completely deaf is believed to be the youngest person to have their hearing restored after undergoing groundbreaking new gene therapy.\n\nSeveral medical teams around the world including in China and the United States have been trialling similar treatments with good results for hereditary deafness that focuses on a rare genetic mutation.\n\nBut UK ear surgeon Manohar Bance said the toddler, Opal, was the first person in the world to receive therapy developed by US biotech firm Regeneron and \"the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know\".\n\nOpal was treated at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, part of Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust, in eastern England.\n\nBance called the results of Opal's surgery \"spectacular -- so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure\".\n\nHe said it came on the back of decades of work and marked \"a new era in the treatment of deafness\".\n\nThe little girl, from Oxfordshire in south central England, has a genetic form of auditory neuropathy, which is caused by the disruption of nerve impulses travelling from the inner ear to the brain.\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which is responsible for making a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve.\n\nTo overcome the fault, the \"new era\" gene therapy from Regeneron delivers a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nBance said that following surgery last September, Opal's hearing was now \"close to normal\" with further improvement expected.\n\nA second child received the gene therapy in Cambridge with positive results seen six weeks after the surgery.\n\n- 'Awe-inspiring' -\n\nChina has been working on targeting the same gene though Bance said theirs used a different technology and slightly different mode of delivery.\n\nMedics in Philadelphia have also reported a good outcome with a type of gene therapy on an 11-year-old boy.\n\nOpal was the first person to take part in a gene therapy trial being carried out in Cambridge by Bance.\n\nThe trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children, including Opal, receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time.\n\nUp to 18 youngsters from the UK, Spain and the United States are being recruited for the trial and will be followed up for five years.\n\nBance said the current treatment for auditory neuropathy was implanted.\n\nPromotedListen to the latest songs, only on JioSaavn.com\n\n\"My entire life, gene therapy has been 'five years away'... to finally see something that actually worked in humans... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really,\" he said.\n\n(Except for the headline, this story has not been edited by NDTV staff and is published from a syndicated feed.)", "source": More deaf children from the UK, Spain and the US are being recruited to the trial and will all be followed up for five years.\n\nProf Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at the trust and chief investigator for the trial, said the initial results were \"better than I hoped or expected\" and could cure patients with this type of deafness.\n\n\"We have results from [Opal] which are very spectacular - so close to normal hearing restoration. So we do hope it could be a potential cure.\"\n\nHe added: \"There's been so much work, decades of work ... to finally see something that actually worked in humans .... It was quite spectacular and a bit awe-inspiring really. It felt very special.\"\n\nAuditory neuropathy can be caused by a fault in the OTOF gene, which makes a protein called otoferlin. This enables cells in the ear to communicate with the hearing nerve. To overcome the fault, the new therapy from biotech firm Regeneron sends a working copy of the gene to the ear.\n\nA second child has also recently received the gene therapy treatment at Cambridge university hospitals, with positive results.\n\nThe overall Chord trial consists of three parts, with three deaf children including Opal receiving a low dose of gene therapy in one ear only.\n\nA different set of three children will get a high dose on one side. Then, if that is shown to be safe, more children will receive a dose in both ears at the same time. In total, 18 children worldwide will be recruited to the trial.\n\nOpal is the first patient globally to receive the therapy and is \"the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know\", Bance said.\n\nThe gene therapy - DB-OTO - is specifically for children with OTOF mutations. A harmless virus is used to carry the working gene into the patient.\n\nThe trial is \"just the beginning of gene therapies\", Bance said. \"It marks a new era in the treatment for deafness.\"\n\nMartin McLean, a senior policy adviser at the National Deaf Children's Society, said deafness should never be a barrier to happiness or fulfilment. \"Many families will welcome these {"uri": "8119091706", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "12:02:39", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T12:02:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T12:02:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8078431487083435, "url": "https://www.firstpost.com/health/worlds-first-gene-therapy-trial-how-a-uk-toddler-born-deaf-can-now-hear-13769057.html", "title": "World's first gene therapy trial: How a UK toddler born deaf can now hear", "body": "Opal Sandy (centre) from Oxfordshire, UK with her mother and father Jo and James Sandy. NHS\n\nOpal Sandy, now an 18-month-old toddler from Oxfordshire, England, has become the first patient to undergo a groundbreaking gene therapy trial for auditory neuropathy, a rare genetic condition causing deafness. Opal's journey from complete deafness to responding to her parents' voices and uttering her first words has captured global attention.\n\nOpal was born deaf due to a fault in the OTOF gene, which disrupts nerve impulses essential for transmitting sounds to the brain. She became the first participant in the CHORD trial, a global initiative aiming to explore the efficacy of gene therapy in restoring hearing for children with auditory neuropathy.\n\nExpressing her joy, Opal's mother Jo Sandy said, \"When Opal could first hear us clapping unaided it was mind-blowing - we were so happy when the clinical team confirmed at 24 weeks that her hearing was also picking up softer sounds and speech. The phrase 'near normal' hearing was used and everyone was so excited such amazing results had been achieved.\"\n\nWhat does the treatment comprise?\n\nAdministered at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, the trial involved infusing Opal's right ear with a harmless virus carrying a functional copy of the OTOF gene, reported the NHS website.\n\n\"These results are spectacular and better than I expected. Gene therapy has been the future in otology and audiology for many years and I'm so excited that it is now finally here. This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the inner ear and many types of hearing loss,\" said Professor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon at Cambridge University Hospitals NHS Foundation Trust and chief investigator of the trial.\n\nOpal is the first patient globally to receive the therapy and is \"the youngest globally that's been done to date as far as we know\", Bance said.\n\nAlso Read: World's first patient begins sickle cell gene therapy treatment: What is it?\n\nBance added, \"We have a short time frame to intervene because of the rapid pace of brain development at this age. Delays in the diagnosis can also cause confusion for families as the many reasons for delayed speech and late intervention can impact a children's development.\"\n\nIn the initial phase of the study, participants are administered a minimal dosage targeted at one ear. Subsequently, in the second phase, a higher dosage is anticipated for unilateral gene therapy, contingent upon the established safety of the initial dosage.\n\nThe third phase will evaluate the efficacy of gene therapy in both ears, with the dosage determined based on safety and effectiveness assessments from the preceding phases. Follow-up appointments spanning five years will monitor patients' progress in adapting to speech comprehension over the long term.\n\nThe treatment showed promising results\n\nWithin weeks of the gene therapy infusion, Opal began responding to sounds, even with her cochlear implant switched off. Continuous improvement was observed in her hearing, culminating in near-normal hearing levels for soft sounds within 24 weeks post-treatment.\n\nOpal's parents, Jo and James Sandy, expressed their elation at witnessing her newfound ability to engage with the world of sound.\n\n\"It was our ultimate goal for Opal to hear all the speech sounds. It's already making a difference to our day-to-day lives, like at bath-time or swimming, when Opal can't wear her cochlear implant. We feel so proud to have contributed to such pivotal findings, which will hopefully help other children like Opal and their families in the future,\" said Opal's father James Sandy.\n\nWhat does this trial mean for the future?\n\nThe success of Opal's treatment heralds a new era in the management of genetic hearing disorders. Professor Bance emphasised the potential of gene therapy as an alternative to conventional treatments like cochlear implants, particularly for conditions linked to specific genetic mutations.\n\nOpal's 24-week results, alongside other scientific data from the CHORD trial are being presented at the American Society of Gene and Cell Therapy (ASGC) in Baltimore, USA this week, reported UK's NHS.\n\nAlso Read: 5-month-old in UK suffers cardiac arrest: How common is this heart ailment in children?\n\nDr Richard Brown, Consultant Paediatrician said, \"The development of genomic medicine and alternative treatments is vital for patients worldwide, and increasingly offers hope to children with previously incurable disorders.\"\n\nAs the CHORD trial progresses, researchers anticipate further insights into the long-term effectiveness and safety of gene therapy for auditory neuropathy. With ongoing enrollment in the US, UK, and Spain, the study aims to explore escalating doses of gene therapy and its potential application in bilateral treatment.\n\nThe establishment of genomic centres of excellence, as envisioned in the forthcoming Cambridge Children's Hospital, underscores the commitment to advancing precision medicine on a global scale.", "source": {"uri": {"uri": "8119144074", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "12:37:16", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T12:37:16Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T12:36:59Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.800000011920929, "url": "https://www.businessinsider.com/deaf-toddler-opal-sandy-hearing-restored-gene-therapy-trial-2024-5", "title": "A deaf toddler can hear thanks to a world-first gene treatment that took just 16 minutes", "body": "A deaf toddler has had her hearing restored after being the first patient treated in a gene therapy trial.\n\nOpal Sandy, who is 18 months old, has auditory neuropathy, a condition caused by a faulty gene.\n\nThe condition means the ear can detect sound but doesn't transmit it to the brain properly. It can cause degrees of hearing loss, but in Opal's case, she was fully deaf from birth.\n\nNow Opal, who lives in Oxfordshire in England, can hear almost normally thanks to a 16-minute long gene therapy procedure.\n\nDoctors injected Opal's right ear with a harmless virus that carried a working version of the faulty gene while she was under general anesthesia.\n\nWithin four weeks of receiving the gene therapy, Opal could respond to sound. After 24 weeks, she had hearing levels that were \"close to normal,\" according to a press release from Britain's health service.\n\nShe can hear whispering in her treated ear, respond to her parents' voices, and has even begun saying words such as \"daddy\" and \"bye-bye\" herself.\n\nAround 1 in 500 babies are born with hearing loss or develop it in early childhood, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention in the US. 50 to 60% of this hearing loss is due to genetic causes.\n\nThe World Health Organization estimates that unaddressed hearing loss represents a global cost of $980 billion a year in costs such as healthcare and educational support.\n\nAuditory neuropathy is caused by a faulty gene and isn't usually detected until a child is 2 to 3 years old, when delays in speech are more noticeable. But Opal's was picked up early through gene testing, which she was eligible for because her older sister has the condition.\n\nUsually, auditory neuropathy is treated with implants, but Opal was put on a trial at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge, England, which is trialing gene therapy on children.\n\nHer treatment was part of phase one of the trial, where children are given a low dose in one ear. Phase two will involve a higher dose, still in one ear, and phase three will move on to treating both ears at a dose deemed safe from the previous phases.\n\nOpal was the first patient, treated in September 2023 when she was 11 months old. A second child received the therapy and has seen good results six weeks post-treatment, according to a release from Regeneron, the pharmaceutical company that makes the infusion.\n\nProfessor Manohar Bance, an ear surgeon and chief investigator of the trial, said the results were \"spectacular and better than I expected.\"\n\n\"This is hopefully the start of a new era for gene therapies for the {"uri": "2024-05-349577368", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-08", "time": "23:08:18", "dateTime": "2024-05-08T23:08:18Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-08T23:01:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7921568751335144, "url": "https://uk.news.yahoo.com/couple-joy-deaf-daughter-hears-230100104.html", "title": "Couple's joy as deaf daughter hears thanks to new gene therapy", "body": "The parents of an 18-month-old girl who can hear thanks to a groundbreaking gene therapy for deafness have said they are delighted with her progress.\n\nJo Sandy, a 33-year-old secondary school geography teacher, and her husband James, 33, who works in car manufacturing, said they were \"gobsmacked\" when they realised Opal could hear without the need for a cochlear implant.\n\nOpal now loves slamming her cutlery on the table to make a noise and enjoys playing with toy drums, a piano and wooden blocks.\n\nThe family, from Oxfordshire, have an older daughter Nora, aged five, who has the same genetic form of auditory neuropathy as Opal and wears cochlear implants, which is the current gold standard treatment.\n\nMrs Sandy told the PA news agency the couple found out that Nora was deaf when she was nine months old and medics said any subsequent children would be entitled to additional hearing tests at birth.\n\n\"Although Nora and Opal passed the newborn hearing screening, which picks up the majority of deafness, when Opal was a newborn she went for the additional testing and we found out she was deaf when she was four days old,\" she said.\n\n\"Nora had her cochlear implants on both sides fitted at 15 months old and following that comes quite an intense rehabilitation process of speech and language therapy and audiologist appointments.\n\n\"She's done really well learning to speak and has managed to close the language gap with her peers.\n\n\"So, hearing that Opal was deaf - of course there was a grieving process that we went through the same as when we found out that Nora was as well - but Nora had set the bar really high and we knew what was possible with lots of hard work and support from lots of people.\"\n\nThe couple first heard about the CHORD gene therapy trial from the ear, nose and throat surgeon at their local John Radcliffe Hospital in Oxford.\n\nThe surgeon knew of Professor Manohar Bance's work and that he was running a trial using a gene therapy from biotech firm Regeneron at Addenbrooke's Hospital in Cambridge.\n\n\"Our initial reaction was we were really nervous,\" Mrs Sandy said, adding it sounded \"too good to be true\".\n\nShe said: \"We were quite nervous to go down a different path to one that we knew had already worked so well for our eldest daughter. But it also sounded like a really unique opportunity.\n\n\"We sort of had a relatively reliable safety net that, even if it didn't work, Opal would still be eligible to have a cochlear implant in her other ear six months down the line.\n\n\"So that was like a really good sort of safety blanket that we could fall back on if we needed to.\"\n\nOpal received a gene therapy infusion in her right ear during surgery last September. At the same time, a cochlear implant was fitted in her left ear to ensure she had hearing.\n\nThere was then a tense wait to see if the therapy had worked, but it became apparent quite quickly that Opal could hear.\n\n\"It was about three weeks post-surgery, which was about a week after her implant had been turned on,\" Mrs Sandy explained.\n\n\"So we were sort of in the routine of testing quite loud sounds like banging, clapping, wooden spoons on saucepans, that kind of really intermittent loud noise.\n\n\"I was testing that with her implant on and hadn't realised that her implant had actually come off, and she turned to pretty loud clapping. When she first turned, I couldn't believe it.\n\n\"I thought it was a fluke or like a change in light or something that had caught her eye, but I repeated it a few times.\n\n\"I picked my phone up and texted James, and said 'I think it's working'. I was absolutely gobsmacked. I thought it was a fluke.\"\n\nThe couple had been told they might notice a change within the first six weeks, hence Mrs Sandy's surprise.\n\n\"I couldn't really believe it,\" she said. \"It was ... bonkers.\"\n\nShe added that \"there is no way in a million years I thought that Opal would be able to turn to sound without wearing an implant\".\n\nSome 24 weeks after surgery, in February this year, tests in Cambridge showed Opal could also hear soft sounds such as a whisper.\n\n\"The audiologist played back some of the sounds that she was responding to and they were ridiculously quiet sort of sounds that in the real world wouldn't catch your attention during a conversation,\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\nNow, even without the implant in her left ear, Opal can hear perfectly well thanks to the gene therapy.\n\nMrs Sandy said: \"Without an implant, she can understand basically the same things that she can understand when it's on, so 'Opal, where's your nose? 'Where's daddy?' 'Who's at the door?' 'Bye bye' ...sort of basic language acquisition, that she can understand just as well with her implant on and off.\"\n\nMr Sandy said he noticed a \"massive\" improvement in the 18 to 24 weeks post-surgery and the \"big moment\" was hearing from the team at Cambridge University Hospitals that Opal had near-normal hearing at 24 weeks.\n\nMrs Sandy added: \"Certainly since February, we've noticed her sister waking her up in the morning because she's running around on the landing, or someone rings on the door so her nap's cut short.\n\n\"She's definitely responding more to sort of what we would call functional sounds rather than just sounds that we use to test her.\n\n\"We were told she had near normal hearing last time - I think they got responses at sort of 25 to 30 decibels.\n\n\"I think normal hearing is classed at 20 decibels, so she's not far off. Before, she had no hearing whatsoever.\"\n\nOpal has also started speaking in the last six weeks.\n\n\"She's good at all your common first baby words, so 'daddy' is a favourite, 'uh oh', 'bye bye',\" Mrs Sandy said.\n\nAs for playing, Mrs Sandy said both Nora and Opal like \"seeing who can make the most noise\".\n\nShe joked: \"I always said I'd never get annoyed with them making noise and I do get annoyed with them making noise.\n\n\"So, Opal loves playing with her little musical instrument set ... playing the drums, playing her little piano, tapping some of her wooden blocks and things like that.\n\n\"She's started becoming more interested in books, so like lots of lift the flap books, 'where's Spot?'...those kinds of really interactive books, she really likes.\n\n\"She loves slamming her cutlery on the table asking us where her dinner is.\n\n\"Nora got into music quite recently and (Opal) likes put her arms up and does little dances in the kitchen.\n\n\"So they like dancing together.
